<li id="cite&#95;note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFSen2014" class="citation cs2">Sen, Colleen Taylor (2014), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VN_vCgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA164"><i>Feasts and Fasts: A History of Food in India</i></a>, Reaktion Books, pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">164–</span>5, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-78023-391-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-78023-391-8"><bdi>978-1-78023-391-8</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Feasts+and+Fasts%3A+A+History+of+Food+in+India&amp;rft.pages=164-5&amp;rft.pub=Reaktion+Books&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-78023-391-8&amp;rft.aulast=Sen&amp;rft.aufirst=Colleen+Taylor&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DVN&#95;vCgAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA164&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APilaf" class="Z3988"></span> Quote: "(pp. 164–165) "Descriptions of the basic technique appear in thirteenth-century Arab cookbooks, although the name pulao is not used. The word itself is medieval Farsi, and the dish may have been created in the early sixteenth century at the Safavid court in Persia.  ... Although dishes combining rice, meat and spices were prepared in ancient times, the technique of first sautéing the rice in ghee and then cooking it slowly to keep the grains separate probably came later with the Mughals."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFPerry1994" class="citation cs2">Perry, Charles (15 December 1994), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.latimes.com/archives/la-xpm-1994-12-15-fo-9100-story.html">"Annual Cookbook Issue: Book Review: An Armchair Guide to the Indian Table: Indian Food: A Historical Companion By K. T. Achaya (Oxford University Press: 1994; $35; 290 pp.)"</a>, <i>Los Angeles Times</i></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Los+Angeles+Times&amp;rft.atitle=Annual+Cookbook+Issue%3A+Book+Review%3A+An+Armchair+Guide+to+the+Indian+Table%3A+Indian+Food%3A+A+Historical+Companion+By+K.+T.+Achaya+%28Oxford+University+Press%3A+1994%3B+%2435%3B+290+pp.%29&amp;rft.date=1994-12-15&amp;rft.aulast=Perry&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.latimes.com%2Farchives%2Fla-xpm-1994-12-15-fo-9100-story.html&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APilaf" class="Z3988"></span> Quote: "The other flaw is more serious. Achaya has clearly read a lot about Indian food, but it was in what historians call secondary sources. In other words, he's mostly reporting what other people have concluded from the primary evidence. Rarely, if ever, does he go to the original data to verify their conclusions.  This is a dangerous practice, particularly in India, because certain Indian scholars like to claim that everything in the world originated in India a long time ago. ... Achaya even invents one or two myths of his own. He says there is evidence that south Indians were making pilaf 2,000 years ago, but if you look up the book he footnotes, you find that the Old Tamil word pulavu had nothing to do with pilaf. It meant raw meat or fish."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite class="citation cs2"><a href="/wiki/Ashis_Nandy" title="Ashis Nandy">Nandy, Ashis</a> (2004), "The Changing Popular Culture of Indian Food: Preliminary Notes", <i>South Asia Research</i>, <b>24</b> (1): <span class="nowrap">9–</span>19, <a href="/wiki/CiteSeerX_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="CiteSeerX (identifier)">CiteSeerX</a>&#160;<span class="id-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://citeseerx.ist.psu.edu/viewdoc/summary?doi=10.1.1.830.7136">10.1.1.830.7136</a></span>, <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1177%2F0262728004042760">10.1177/0262728004042760</a>, <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/issn/0262-7280">0262-7280</a>, <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:143223986">143223986</a></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=South+Asia+Research&amp;rft.atitle=The+Changing+Popular+Culture+of+Indian+Food%3A+Preliminary+Notes&amp;rft.volume=24&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=9-19&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fciteseerx.ist.psu.edu%2Fviewdoc%2Fsummary%3Fdoi%3D10.1.1.830.7136%23id-name%3DCiteSeerX&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A143223986%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.issn=0262-7280&amp;rft&#95;id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F0262728004042760&amp;rft.aulast=Nandy&amp;rft.aufirst=Ashis&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APilaf" class="Z3988"></span> Quote: " (p. 11) Not merely ingredients came to the subcontinent, but also recipes. ... All around India one finds preparations that came originally from outside South Asia. <i>Kebabs</i> came from West and Central Asia and underwent radical metamorphosis in the hot and dusty plains of India. So did <i>biryani</i> and <i>pulao</i>, two rice preparations, usually with meat. Without them, ceremonial dining in many parts of India, Pakistan, and Bangladesh is incomplete. Even the term <i>pulao</i> or <i>pilav</i> seems to have come from Arabic and Persian. It is true that in Sanskrit — in the Yajnavalkya Smriti — and in old Tamil, the term <i>pulao</i> occurs (Achaya, 1998b: 11), but it is also true that <i>biryani</i> and <i>pulao</i> today carry mainly the stamp of the Mughal times and its Persianized high culture.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-nabhan-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-nabhan_29-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-nabhan_29-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFNabhan2014" class="citation book cs1">Nabhan, Gary Paul (2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=e-glDQAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA135"><i>Cumin, Camels, and Caravans: A Spice Odyssey</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_Press" title="University of California Press">University of California Press</a>. p.&#160;135. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0520267206" title="Special:BookSources/978-0520267206"><bdi>978-0520267206</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Cumin%2C+Camels%2C+and+Caravans%3A+A+Spice+Odyssey&amp;rft.pages=135&amp;rft.pub=University+of+California+Press&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-0520267206&amp;rft.aulast=Nabhan&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary+Paul&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3De-glDQAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA135&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APilaf" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pilaf&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1327269900" /><div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns reflist-columns-2"><ol class="references" data-mw-group="note">
<li id="cite&#95;note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Oxford English Dictionary Online Edition (subscription required): pilaf (n) = pilau n.1; pilau n.1 = "A dish, partly of Middle Eastern, partly and ultimately of South Asian origin, consisting of rice (or, in certain areas, wheat) cooked in stock with spices, usually mixed with meat and various other ingredients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pilauOED&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pilauOED-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Merriam-Webster Unabridged Dictionary (subscription required): "rice usually combined with meat and vegetables, fried in oil, steamed in stock, and seasoned with any of numerous herbs (as saffron or cumin)."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster&#95;Unabridged&#95;Dictionary2019&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMerriam-Webster_Unabridged_Dictionary2019-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Perry: "A Middle-Eastern method of cooking rice so that every grain remains separate. ...However, there is no evidence that rice was cooked by this technique in India before the Muslim invasions, and Indians themselves associate pilaf-making with Muslim cities such as Hyderabad, Lucknow, and Delhi. .... The first descriptions of the pilaf technique appear in the 13th-century Arabic books Kitab al-Tabikh and Kitab al-Witsla ila al Habib, written in Baghdad and Syria, respectively. They show the technique in its entirety, including the cloth beneath the lid, and describe still-current flavourings such as meat, pulses, and fruit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPerry2014624&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerry2014624-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Roger: "As noted, Iranians have a unique method of preparing rice. This method is designed to leave the grains separate and tasty, making the rice fluffy and very flavorful. After soaking, parboiling, and draining, the rice is poured into a dish smeared with melted butter. The lid is then sealed tightly with a cloth and a paste of flour and water. The last stage is to steam it on low heat for about half an hour, after which the rice is removed and fluffed."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERoger20001144&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoger20001144-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Roger: " (p. 1143) Under the Abbassids, for example (ninth to twelfth century), during the Golden Age of Islam, there was one single empire from Afghanistan to Spain and the North of Arabia. The size of the empire allowed many foods to spread throughout the Middle East. From India, rice went to Syria, Iraq, and Iran, and eventually, it became known and cultivated all the way to Spain. ....  Many dishes of that period are still prepared today with ingredients available to the common people. Some of these are vinegar preserves, roasted meat, and cooked livers, which could be bought in the streets, eaten in the shops, or taken home. Such dishes considerably influenced medieval European and Indian cookery;<i>pilaf</i> and meat patties that started out as <i>samosa</i> or <i>sambusak</i>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERoger20001143&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoger20001143-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-Nandy11-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Nandy11_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nandy: "(p. 11) Not merely ingredients came to the subcontinent, but also recipes. ... All around India one finds preparations that came originally from outside South Asia. <i>Kebabs</i> came from West and Central Asia and underwent radical metamorphosis in the hot and dusty plains of India. So did <i>biryani</i> and <i>pulao</i>, two rice preparations, usually with meat. Without them, ceremonial dining in many parts of India, Pakistan, and Bangladesh is incomplete. Even the term <i>pulao</i> or <i>pilav</i> seems to have come from Arabic and Persian. It is true that in Sanskrit — in the Yajnavalkya Smriti — and in old Tamil, the term <i>pulao</i> occurs (Achaya, 1998b: 11), but it is also true that <i>biryani</i> and <i>pulao</i> today carry mainly the stamp of the Mughal times and its Persianized high culture."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENandy200411&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENandy200411-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-Sengupta74-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Sengupta74_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sengupta: "(p. 74) Muslim influence on the style and substance of Indian food was profound. K.T. Achaya writes that the Muslims imported a new refinement and a courtly etiquette of both group and individual dining into the austere dining ambience of Hindu society. ... Babur's son, Humayun, came back to India after spending a long period of exile in Kabul and the Safavid imperial court in Iran. He brought with him an entourage of Persian cooks who introduced the rich and elaborate rice cookery of the Safavid courts to India, combining Indian spices and Persian arts into a rich fusion that became the iconic dish of Islamic South Asian cuisine, the <i>biryani</i>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESengupta201474&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESengupta201474-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Roger: " (p. 1143) Under the Abbassids, for example (ninth to twelfth century), during the Golden Age of Islam, there was one single empire from Afghanistan to Spain and the North of Arabia. The size of the empire allowed many foods to spread throughout the Middle East. From India, rice went to Syria, Iraq, and Iran, and eventually, it became known and cultivated all the way to Spain. ....  Many dishes of that period are still prepared today with ingredients available to the common people. Some of these are vinegar preserves, roasted meat, and cooked livers, which could be bought in the streets, eaten in the shops, or taken home. Such dishes considerably influenced medieval European and Indian cookery; for example, <i>paella</i>, which evolved from <i>pulao</i>, and <i>pilaf</i> and meat patties that started out as <i>samosa</i> or <i>sambusak</i>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERoger20001143&#95;10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoger20001143-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nandy: "(p. 11) All around India one finds preparations that came originally from outside South Asia. <i>Kebabs</i> came from West and Central Asia and underwent radical metamorphosis in the hot and dusty plains of India. So did <i>biryani</i> and <i>pulao</i>, two rice preparations, usually with meat. Without them, ceremonial dining in many parts of India, Pakistan, and Bangladesh is incomplete."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENandy200411&#95;12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENandy200411-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sengupta: "(p. 74)  Muslim influence on the style and substance of Indian food was profound. K.T. Achaya writes that the Muslims imported a new refinement and a courtly etiquette of both group and individual dining into the austere dining ambience of Hindu society. ... Babur's son, Humayun, came back to India after spending a long period of exile in Kabul and the Safavid imperial court in Iran. He brought with him an entourage of Persian cooks who introduced the rich and elaborate rice cookery of the Safavid courts to India, combining Indian spices and Persian arts into a rich fusion that became the iconic dish of Islamic South Asian cuisine, the <i>biryani</i>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESengupta201474&#95;14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESengupta201474-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
